Some non-free os issue commands to users like this: "hey you, shut down,
restart ..." and the user has to obey as commanded.

Free software gives users the freedom to do what they want with their
system, and they are free to improve an application to suit their need
or requirement.

There is is an ocean of difference between the two, and there is no
point in giving ideas like "hey you, on you knees/keep away ..."  free
software users. Please avoid such posts to this list atleast.

Lengthy forms may use several different data types and  any decent form 
validation script gets bluky, and using  JavaScript for form validation 
is certainly "crappy" as you say.  But the problem is that we have to do 
a lot of dirty work writing "sticky forms", if validation fails on the 
server script.  A partial post and get could avoid crappy code at both ends.

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0410182325440.24766@chi.spunge.org>

Hi,

I have come across a topic called http compression.
It says that any of the web content can be compressed
by the webserver or application server and sent over
network. And according to the http 1.1 standard most
of the browsers will understand the compressed format
and on the fly uncompress and present to the user(without
user's intervention).

This will make the rendering of the html fast and reduce
the time spent for transportation on network.

I heard that apache supports that with additional module
(mod_gzip).

I have Netscape Enterprise Server 4.1.(web server and
not Application Server). I think, this does not support
compression on the fly.

Is there any way by which I can implement the same for
my large html files in my application. If I just add
the header ('Content-Encoding', 'gzip' ), I think it will
not work and I need to add some logic or through API(Servlets
and core java) I shoudl achieve this compression. Am I correct?

Google helped me little. But if somebody can point
me to a more relevant web link or give some suggestions,
that would be great.

Thanks and regards,
carti

Subject: [Ilugc] Partial post or get
In-Reply-To: <41749016.8070004@frodo.hserus.net>
References: <4173F252.5060406@md4.vsnl.net.in>
	<20041018171244.GA13666@symonds.net>
	<41748EC0.4050606@md4.vsnl.net.in> <41749016.8070004@frodo.hserus.net>
Message-ID: <48bf63dc041019024356e0d68f@mail.gmail.com>

  Using multiple form tags in the html will solve the partial submit
issue. When you submit any one of the forms, only the data elements
that is declared in that particular form will be submitted; all the
other form data will be discarded as the control moves to the next
page.

 If server side GET or POST to a different server is what you are
looking for then it can be achieved by using CURL lib in PHP

Regards,
P r a v e e n . P . V



On Tue, 19 Oct 2004 09:25:02 +0530, Suresh Ramasubramanian
<linux@frodo.hserus.net> wrote:
> Ramanraj K wrote:
> > Lengthy forms may use several different data types and  any decent form
> > validation script gets bluky, and using  JavaScript for form validation
> > is certainly "crappy" as you say.  But the problem is that we have to do

From ramanraj at md4.vsnl.net.in  Tue Oct 19 19:21:49 2004

	<41748E82.30209@md4.vsnl.net.in> <41748FC4.3030506@frodo.hserus.net>
Message-ID: <41751BF5.1000707@md4.vsnl.net.in>

Suresh Ramasubramanian wrote:
     > Ramanraj K wrote:
     >
     >> Some non-free os issue commands to users like this:
     >> "hey you, shut down,restart ..." and the user has to
     >> obey as commanded.
     >
     > look, this is complete nonsense.

Yes. It is complete nonsense to do such things.

     > he made a fairly valid point about how having to rely on a
     > mouse with emacs was not going to be productive, given that
     > emacs is basically a CLI based editor, relying on keypress
     > combinations.

Emacs might have started out as a CLI based editor because pointing
devices were not common then, but now, the mouse is well supported for
editing.  In the "Frames and X Windows" section of the Emacs manual, the
use of the mouse is elaborately dealt with.  There is also a chapter on
"mouse-avoidance" for those who think of it as a nuisance. There are
other pages on creating buttons and widgets that make sense within
Emacs. Therefore, the casual comment "If you're using the mouse, you're
better off using something else" is misleading and incorrect.

 > http://interviews.slashdot.org/interviews/04/10/18/1153211.shtml
 >
 > One little known factoid seems to be that he's worked a *lot* on text 
editors
 > in his youth. What's so fascinating about editors that it attracts the
 > best hackers (RMS/EMacs, ...)? All the folks who're fighting about 
CLI/GUI
 > Emacs, etc. would do well to read this article.
 >
 > Anyway, the section on filesystems/databases, some of the CS work at

Sivasankar, thanks for the link to the interesting, frank and open
interview of Rob Pike.  Pointer like this truly guide and help :)

BTW, regardless of the os we use, reading the "The Unix Programming
Environment" [Rs. 125/- !] by Brian W. Kernighan and Rob Pike would be
rewarding. It includes a good introduction to ed at the end.  It seems
sed was written "one afternoon" by modifying ed!  Such superb technical
writings with grace and style are very rare these days, and maybe even
no longer appreciated.  The parable of "The Unix and the Echo" by Doug
McIllroy is reproduced in the book, bringing out the deep connection
between philosophy, specification and implementation :)

Regards,
Ramanraj.

From sangulinux at rediffmail.com  Wed Oct 20 09:55:50 2004
From: sangulinux at rediffmail.com (Sangharsha p g)
Date: Wed Oct 20 15:25:51 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] How the linux kernel is superior than windows kernel?
Message-ID: <20041020095521.10272.qmail@webmail26.rediffmail.com>

 ?

  It is not that it is superior OR inferior, in windows kernel we don't know what is happening inside and there is no previlege for us to customize the kernel of windows for resource conservation.Where as in Linux kernel we have a previlege to know internals of kernel so that we can remove the things in kernel which we don't require(may be blocking thing which which are security holes) I mean to say we can customize the kernel to our requirement.

 >> Emacs might have started out as a CLI based editor because pointing
 >> devices were not common then, but now, the mouse is well supported for
 >> editing.  In the "Frames and X Windows" section of the Emacs manual, the
 >> use of the mouse is elaborately dealt with.  There is also a chapter on
 >> "mouse-avoidance" for those who think of it as a nuisance. There are
 >> other pages on creating buttons and widgets that make sense within
 >> Emacs. Therefore, the casual comment "If you're using the mouse, you're
 >> better off using something else" is misleading and incorrect.
 >
 >
 > emacs supports the mouse is something on an equivalent scale to "emacs
 > has cute, sharp and clear fonts" etc.
 >
 > all these are bolt-ons for what is basically a CLI tool, so that you'd
 > have to do more than the normal amount of work with a mouse for what a
 > single M-[x][y] or C-[x][y] keypress would do for you.

A mouse has its own limitations and does not get any better in other
applications. Even in native GUI applications, key stroke equivalents
for the mouse are mostly available, and users can customise and use
whatever suits them most.  Therefore, let us please keep the poor mouse
out of this issue.

To put things in the right perspective, let us get back to the original
issue. It is plain that Emacs, as of now, does not support listboxes,
combo boxes, toggle buttons, and other GUI widgets, except a few.
Newbies could save a lot of time by not searching for them. May be if
those who really feel the need for it, work on this, we may end up with
a good looking GUI for Emacs.  That could make Emacs a good GUI + CLI
tool, and life a little easier for me too :)

> why the Linux kernel is more stable, secured and flexible than Winows

One major tech up is the Unix like file permissions and ownership
which can be so easily modified and changed. Then there is the vast
amount of tools available all of them again open source and hence
again more secure.

Basically the one point that adds secority to any app is it being open
source. Open source apps can always be tweaked up and looked around.

Hi!

My thoughts below:

There is no doubt Linux is far superior than windoze
in terms of stability, security and performance. 

I would encourage you to read:

[1] Abraham Silberschatz, Peter Baer Galvin & Greg
Gagne. (2003). Operating System Concepts. (6th ed.).
John Wiley & Sons.

If you would like to understand the Linux kernel
internals, then you can look into:

[2] Daniel P. Bovet & Marco Cesati. (January 2004).
Understanding the Linux Kernel. (2nd ed.). O'Reilly.

Linux has an excellent memory management system.
windoze hogs the hardware unnecessarily. 

You might want to check this link too:

Why I hate micro$oft:
http://www.euronet.nl/users/frankvw/rants/microsoft/IhateMS.html

From shvelmur at gmail.com  Wed Oct 20 22:03:05 2004
From: shvelmur at gmail.com (Shivanand Velmurugan)
Date: Wed Oct 20 22:03:26 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] How the linux kernel is superior than windows kernel?
In-Reply-To: <20041020160623.C250A1DB289@www.ae.iitm.ac.in>
References: <41768455.1000506@frodo.hserus.net>
	<20041020160623.C250A1DB289@www.ae.iitm.ac.in>
Message-ID: <e1f1a51304102009336c979412@mail.gmail.com>

Hi all,

I work for a software company in Chennai. I use 2/3 versions Windows,
2/3 versions of Solaris at office and Linux (RHEL) at home.

I've been with chennai lug for about a week now and all I see is a
bunch of flame wars about  how linux is the greatest or windows sucks
etc... there is no end to such issues, unless we get it out of our
mindset, that because we like a particular OS, we should defend it.

I like Suresh Ramasubramanian idea that there should be scietific
evidence and not just a bunch of opinions that determine the laurels
of an give software system.

Further, we shouldn't be looking at the raw kernel power etc.. what is
critical is which OS is best suited to a user, given his requirement.

There is no point in comparing something like Linux and Windows coz,
the linux kernel you guys are talking about is not out there to make
money, but the windows is ... If all the software that you will ever
use is to be free, where do you think that software dev, designer,
PMs, PL, CEOs etc. get their salaries from.. remember also that a
majority of Linux distros that are easily usables by most users are
paid versions. Btw, any of you use RHEL/ Suse etc, without the
licensing is required, then you are still violating exactly the same
rights that any guy using an illegal version of windows is .

And no, I do not say that Windows is the greatest or Linux .. there
are for different markets.. maybe as a home user, getting familiar
with Linux is a good bet, since you can get your self liberated from
copyright issues, but then think of a user, say a music composer. He
wants to setup a small home studio where he can do some recording and
arrangements.. there is no way on earth that he can think of something
like linux.. yeah , he can go for a mac, but he can setup something
that delivers the same performance with windows for half that cost. So
both OSes have their place and we shoudl respect that

regs
Shivanand

From lug at chandrashekar.info  Wed Oct 20 17:53:15 2004
From: lug at chandrashekar.info (Chandrashekar Babu)
Date: Wed Oct 20 23:23:58 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] How the linux kernel is superior than windows kernel?
In-Reply-To: <20041020155836.175FB1DB25A@www.ae.iitm.ac.in>
References: <5800e998041020062755306066@mail.gmail.com>
	<20041020155836.175FB1DB25A@www.ae.iitm.ac.in>
Message-ID: <20041020175315.58f92bfb.lug@chandrashekar.info>

> Ever heard of ACLs? Windows has a more granular system of permissions.
> As for security, could you give any specific unpatched example rather
> than making a sweeping generalization?

Linux 2.6 kernel officially supports SELinux (FLASK security model) as a
part of the kernel. This model is much superior when compared to MAC and
ACL security framework used on Windows operating systems. It provides a
more granular set of permissions and access control ruleset. And yes, it
is more powerful in handling untrusted applications by the use of
sandbox/jail like framework. 

Of course, NSA has funded/incubated this project, which means it should
be possible to deploy military grade security framework using
SELinux (better than TrustedSolaris, TrustedBSD and so on). If thats not
enough... there are many more alternatives (Linux Capability Set,
GRSecurity, Immunix-LSM, RSBAC and so on) to sort out the issue of
default UNIX DAC security model.

Talking broadly from the security perspective, I haven't come across
much options for securing the Windows platforms especially from
untrusted applications. There is no option on Windows platform infact,
to replace the default MAC security model with DBAC, RSBAC, FLASK or
anything better. 


Cheers,
Chandrashekar Babu.

From rsubr at pcomm.hfi.unimelb.edu.au  Thu Oct 21 05:12:16 2004
From: rsubr at pcomm.hfi.unimelb.edu.au (Raja Subramanian)
Date: Thu Oct 21 05:01:41 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] How the linux kernel is superior than windows kernel?
In-Reply-To: <41762FB2.6070200@tandbintl.com>
References: <41762FB2.6070200@tandbintl.com>
Message-ID: <20041020234216.GB6609@pcomm.hfi.unimelb.edu.au>

Hi,

T.KaruppuSwamy (T & B) wrote:
> I am using Windows for last 6 years and Linux for last 6 months. I able
> to find good improvement of windows kernel stability over the period of
> Windows NT to Windows XP. But we know that everyone claims that Linux
> kernel is more superior than Windows kernel. I am reading document on
> net  regarding this.

> Can anybody give me technical explanation *(i.e kernel designwise)* on
> why the Linux kernel is more stable, secured and flexible than Winows
> kernel?

Enough has been said about linux in this thread already, so I'll risk taking
the NT side of things ;-)


 From a pure architectural perspective, the Windows NT kernel is remarkable.
It's a hybrid microkernel that targets the stability and flexibility of a pure
microkernel system while retaining the performance of a monolithic kernel.

The NT kernel consists of two parts - the core kernel and various "subsystems"
that depend on the core kernel.  Subsystems run in kernel mode and perform a
variety of tasks - virtual memory management, cache manager, the user mode
API libraries[1], etc.  The kernel is very flexible can be "extended" in
amazing ways by creating new subsystems.

In recent years, the kernel has given in to "feature creep" and the number of
subsystems has steadily increased in quantiy and complexity[2].  Since buggy
subsystems have the potential to crash the entire system, NT is becoming
more and more unstable.

Another instability problem occurs due to buggy drivers.  NT drivers run
partly inside the core kernel and partly as subsystems in the Executive layer.
Buggy drivers can easily lock up NT.  I used to run WinNT4.0 for ~18 months
and never had a single crash - it's pretty stable if you install good drivers
for your hw.


Linus has done an excellent job in keeping unnecessary stuff out.  The khttpd
and Tux projects have embedded a http server into the kernel.  But thankfully,
they have not been adopted widely as Linux grew the sendfile syscall to allow
even user mode http/ftp/etc servers to accomplish the same thing.

I guess Linux's "superiority" arises from good design decisions.



[1] NT supports win32, POSIX and OS/2 subsystems natively.  Dos/Win9x
    emulation is also done the same way.  Ofcourse, POSIX and OS/2 apis are
    poorly implemented to favour win32.  In the initial release, POSIX
    subsystem did not even support networking.  MS sure knows how to kill the
    competition!

[2] NT 4.0 and above runs the GUI subsystem in kernel mode.  This caused Dave
    Cutler the NT tech lead to quit MS.  With Longhorn, MS definitely move its
    latest database like FS into the kernel... an in-kernel RDBMS engine!

- Raja

From ksriram at gmx.net  Thu Oct 21 06:23:21 2004
From: ksriram at gmx.net (Sriram Krishnan)
Date: Thu Oct 21 06:25:08 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] How the linux kernel is superior than windows kernel?
In-Reply-To: <20041020234216.GB6609@pcomm.hfi.unimelb.edu.au>
Message-ID: <20041021005504.4EC151DB284@www.ae.iitm.ac.in>

Earlier in this thread, there was a discussion about ACLs and Windows vs
Linux security models by Chandrashekar. I mailed a MS employee and here's
the response I got

Sriram,

  First off, while it's wonderful that there's an experimental derivative of
Linux that is adding mandatory access checks, none of the mainline
distributions actually implement this functionality.  There are lots of
experiments going on out there, but until the results of those experiments
reach a mainline branch, it's not particularly relevant to the real world.
As far as I know, the mainline Linux 2.6 branch doesn't include all of the
SELinux hooks, just some of them.   And none of them are enabled on any of
the mainline distributions.

 

  In addition, the acid test of an authorization system occurs when the
filesystems and other components are rev'ed to add this support.  Just
because the kernel has support for a feature doesn't mean that end-users get
to see the benefit of that feature.  How many existing applications will
have to be redesigned when the authorization mechanism changes from WGO to
the SELinux role based access check mechanism?

 

Windows ACL model is built into every single Windows NT system, and thus
every application gets to take advantage of it.  Windows supports an
extraordinarily diverse set of security mechanisms.  For example, if you
want to implement a *nix style WGO access check mechanism, you can (check
out this for some details on how to do it).  Windows NT supports pluggable
authentication models.  Natively, Windows supports at least two different
network authentication systems (Kerberos and NTLM), with widely different
authentication semantics.  There are third party vendors who have
implemented their own authentication mechanism, and they've been doing it
since 1994.

 

All of these capabilities have been in Windows NT since 1993 when it was
first released.

 

Since Windows 2000, Windows has supported two-part authorization (smart
cards and biometrics), which added a third native authentication system.
This functionality was dropped into the system without changing the
documented security semantics.  That's the beauty of a system where the
security infrastructure was well designed, well isolated, and built in from
day one.

 

 

Btw, In the interest of fairness, if you implement the WGO stuff I described
in the ACL conversion post, then there ARE some things that will be broken.
In particular, the CACLS tool and the built in NT ACL UI don't handle
Exchange Canonical ACLs very well.  But if the application treats the ACLs
on objects as opaque blobs, they work perfectly, without modification.

From siva at dias.ath.cx  Thu Oct 21 07:56:14 2004
From: siva at dias.ath.cx (Sivasankar Chander)
Date: Thu Oct 21 08:21:49 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: Microkernels
Message-ID: <20041021022615.116D0329BE@whitebox.dias.ath.cx>

> 
>  From a pure architectural perspective, the Windows NT kernel is remarkable.
> It's a hybrid microkernel that targets the stability and flexibility of a pure
> microkernel system while retaining the performance of a monolithic kernel.
> 
> The NT kernel consists of two parts - the core kernel and various "subsystems"
> that depend on the core kernel.  Subsystems run in kernel mode and perform a
> variety of tasks - virtual memory management, cache manager, the user mode
> API libraries[1], etc.  The kernel is very flexible can be "extended" in
> amazing ways by creating new subsystems.
> 
  Thanks for the informative post. I'll add some historical perspective
to show how MS (and everybody else who dabbled in Microkernels in the
early 90s) were led astray by the same fallacy.

  The common idea behind all microkernels was to have a simple, throughly
tested and correct microkernel with minimal extraneous code, running at
the highest privilege level; and all additional stuff (including traditional
kernel functionality like I/O, drivers, paging, scheduler policies, etc.)
at a lower privilege outside the microkernel, sometimes as simple user-mode
processes. The various implementations differed in details - some used
system calls to interface to the microkernel, while others like Mach (the
grand-daddy of all microkernels) used message-passing.

  All three microkernels in use today (Windows NT, Mac OS X and Hurd) have
a strong Mach heritage. Windows NT through Rick Rashid & Dave Cutler, OS X
through Avi Tevanian (CMU, NeXT), Jim Magee (IBM Microkernel), etc., and Hurd
directly through Mach (Utah & CMU).

  All three made the same mistake: They assumed that by moving complexity
to user-mode (or a lower privilege level), it would be easier to manage,
debug and maintain. Nothing could be further from the truth! Spaghetti is
spaghetti, whether it's in-kernel or out-of-kernel. A problem in a device
driver or a root name server can bring the entire system down - it's
immaterial if the kernel is still running, if the rest of the system is
down. In reality, a microkernel only succeeded in creating multiple
points of failure, with Metcalfe's law making it much more difficult to
pinpoint the exact sequence that caused the failure - debugging became
harder, not easier!

  Anyway, this realization caused IBM to halt its own microkernel project
(also based on Mach) in the mid-90s, and the only other Unix-related
microkernel project, OSF/1, also remained a showpiece with little use.
The Hurd developers ran into this same issue much later, as did Apple
and MS. MS attempted to solve the problem by moving stuff back into the
microkernel, which then ceased to remain a microkernel. Apple, OTOH, has
done an outstanding job in releasing a stable microkernel - but then again,
it had a great development team who had previously worked on two different
Mach-related projects, NeXTStep and Workplace, so they knew what worked
and what to avoid. They also had full control over their hardware platform,
so additional low-level drivers are minimal (mostly video cards, etc.).

  MS does not have this luxury, so it's no surprise that NT driver stability
is the issue that takes down NT frequently.

  All of this is generally moot, since the Linux kernel has achieved *both*
excellent driver availability and stability (in addition to all its other
features), so there's no longer any need to consider any other alternative,
except perhaps the *BSDs, which still rock.

-Siva

From siva at dias.ath.cx  Thu Oct 21 07:59:35 2004
From: siva at dias.ath.cx (Sivasankar Chander)
Date: Thu Oct 21 08:25:08 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] SELinux, ACLs, etc.

> 
>   First off, while it's wonderful that there's an experimental derivative of
> Linux that is adding mandatory access checks, none of the mainline
> distributions actually implement this functionality.  There are lots of
> experiments going on out there, but until the results of those experiments
> reach a mainline branch, it's not particularly relevant to the real world.
> As far as I know, the mainline Linux 2.6 branch doesn't include all of the
> SELinux hooks, just some of them.   And none of them are enabled on any of
> the mainline distributions.
> 
  FUD. SELinux has been available in userland since FC1, and all you need
to do is to modify the policy framework to integrate aditional apps - there
is minimal additional dependancy for most apps.

AFAIK, the kernel SELinux support is a complete merge from the NSA codebase.
>  
> How many existing applications will
> have to be redesigned when the authorization mechanism changes from WGO to
> the SELinux role based access check mechanism?
> 
  It's better to migrate the apps methodically to an SELinux policy-based
mechanism, rather than change everything overnight, as Longhorn is attempting
to do. Over time, the Linux model will work better, as it offers a larger
ensemble of choices, including whether to use SELinux or not.

> 
> Windows NT supports pluggable
> authentication models.  Natively, Windows supports at least two different
> network authentication systems (Kerberos and NTLM), with widely different
> authentication semantics.  There are third party vendors who have
> implemented their own authentication mechanism, and they've been doing it
> since 1994.
> 
  ACLs, PAM, Kerberos, etc. are all derived from OSS- and Unix implementations,
and have been standard on GNU/Linux for an eternity. Don't even get me
started on how MS tried to break Kerberos compatibility by their usual
embrace-and-extend strategy.

>  
> Since Windows 2000, Windows has supported two-part authorization (smart
> cards and biometrics), which added a third native authentication system.
> This functionality was dropped into the system without changing the
> documented security semantics.  That's the beauty of a system where the
> security infrastructure was well designed, well isolated, and built in from
> day one.
> 
  You're joking, right? You actually consider smart-cards and biometrics
as *major* architectural implementations? These mechanisms were always there
in Linux (or any *ix or *BSD) using the PAM framework, and all it takes
is a single PAM object for each mechanism. BTW, PAM came from Sun Microsystems
and MS has nothing to do with it.

  OK, try doing this - use a single PAM object to setup and detach an
encrypted filesystem, on any kind of block device, on just about any
kernel version dating from 2001 to today, using just about any well-known
encryption algorithm (DES, 3DES, MARS, Blowfish, Twofish, AES, ...),
and still remain on-disk compatible across all the kernel levels. (This is
possible today using cryptoloop on the Linux kernel - I just migrated
to Linux-2.6.8.1 with no changes to my encrypted filesystems or my userland
PAM framework).

-Siva

Dear Friends,

Thanks for your overwhelming response/discussion based on thread I 
started. Really it is a amazing that people are learning more and more 
keep them updated. I want to mention some points here to summarize the 
flow of this thread! Since I am not a system programmer, whatever I have 
understood I am summarizing here.

1. Actually I asked about kernel design level comparison, but the thread 
flew in different direction mostly. So I have decided to take up this as 
assignment soon and make a simple document comparing the kernel features 
(definitely I willl have tough time.. of course enjoyable too.. to read 
good books). Based on that we can start a thread, I think that would be 
more scientifc approach than some emotional talks.

2. I thank Mr. Sivanand VelMurugan for his explanation:

>but then think of a user, say a music composer. He
>wants to setup a small home studio where he can do some recording and
>arrangements.. there is no way on earth that he can think of something
>like linux.. yeah , he can go for a mac, but he can setup something
>that delivers the same performance with windows for half that cost. So
>both OSes have their place and we shoudl respect that
>
You are 100% right!

3. I thank Mr. Raja Subramaniam for the following valuable inputs. I 
agree with this, I too experienced many times.

>Another instability problem occurs due to buggy drivers.  NT drivers run
>partly inside the core kernel and partly as subsystems in the Executive layer.
>Buggy drivers can easily lock up NT.  I used to run WinNT4.0 for ~18 months
>and never had a single crash - it's pretty stable if you install good drivers
>for your hw.
>

Thanks to all for this wonderful thread! Let us have more scientific 
discussion!

Regards
T.KaruppuSwamy

Vijairaj  R wrote: 
,----
| I  thought  Sendmail  and  Apache  were primarily  written  for  Linux
| flavours and  later ported to windows.  So will it be  fair to Compare
| their performance  on Win  & Linux??? Moreover  Sendmail &  Apache are
| open source and against the M$ ideology and close relatives to linux.
`----

Why not write  your own "simple" C code that runs  on both windows and
linux and  compile it  in the OS's  respective "style"  and benchmark.
That  way it becomes  scientific, no?   So the  argument of  'this was
written for  windows/linux first' can be  ruled out and it  might be a
fair contest.

You can  start by writing  'search' algorithms, 'sort'  algorithms and
such and compare the results.  You might not even need two boxes to do
this test.  A dual-boot  box should do  (unless I'm missing  some fine
point here).

cheers,

  -Suraj

On Thu, Oct 21, 2004 at 09:52:56AM +0530, Suraj Kumar wrote:
> 
> Why not write  your own "simple" C code that runs  on both windows and
> linux and  compile it  in the OS's  respective "style"  and benchmark.
> That  way it becomes  scientific, no?   So the  argument of  'this was
> written for  windows/linux first' can be  ruled out and it  might be a
> fair contest.
> 
> You can  start by writing  'search' algorithms, 'sort'  algorithms and
> such and compare the results.  You might not even need two boxes to do
> this test.  A dual-boot  box should do  (unless I'm missing  some fine
> point here).
> 

The performance of `search', `sort' algorithms, etc. depends more on the
programmer and the compiler rather than the OS. These algorithms are CPU
(and somewhat) memory intensive. We should test real benchmarks, the
performance of which we care about.

Suriya

PS: If at all, the benchmarking which we are talking about is going to proceed
	--- why not test the performance (and other parameters) of both Apache
	and IIS on Windows, rather than fight over which one is to be used?

From siva at dias.ath.cx  Thu Oct 21 11:10:54 2004
From: siva at dias.ath.cx (Sivasankar Chander)
Date: Thu Oct 21 11:36:34 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] How the linux kernel is superior than windows kernel?
In-Reply-To: <e1f1a51304102009336c979412@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20041021054054.1C9BE329BE@whitebox.dias.ath.cx>

> 
> 
> the linux kernel you guys are talking about is not out there to make
> money, but the windows is
>
  The kernel is not able to make money, but neither is Windows - both are
inanimate. If you mean that those who sell Windows bundled with their
hardware make money, there is nothing that prevents them from doing 
the same with OSS, and in fact a number of companies do so - IBM,
HP, Acer, etc. all bundle Linux distributions with some of their
hardware on request. 

> ... If all the software that you will ever
> use is to be free, where do you think that software dev, designer,
> PMs, PL, CEOs etc. get their salaries from..

  It's free as in free speech, not free as in free lunch. If you mean
that margins will get destroyed by software commoditization, that's
even more true about Windows. Numerous independent producers of
tools, compilers, databases, word processors, spreadsheets, etc. have
been put out of business, along with all the software developers, etc.,
by the integration of applications into the standard windows package.

  If at all anything, the spread of OSS tends to prevent commoditization
of software, since the ability to customize OSS like Sendmail, Apache,
PHP, MySQL, etc. creates huge opportunities at the next meta-layer. 
Witness the birth of hugely profitable businesses like Google, EBay, etc.
that use OSS to create profitable niches in the next layer that cannot be
commoditized easily. 

  If you mean that software developers who develop for the Windows platform
are going to be out of jobs, I'd agree with you. Tough luck - there's no
entitlement to salaries that anybody gets by divine right. People who
made horse-drawn carriages were left without jobs when the automobile
came along. Some, who adapted by making coachwork for saloon cars, still
made a lot of money and survive to this day. Adapt or perish - it's the
survival of the fittest.

> Btw, any of you use RHEL/ Suse etc, without the
> licensing is required, then you are still violating exactly the same
> rights that any guy using an illegal version of windows is .

  Wrong. You may violate copyright when you make a copy for redistribution,
but you do not violate it when merely *using* binaries derived from GPLed code,
even if the binaries are part of an illegally copied distribution. Read
this carefully, and understand it well. Copyright is all about the right
to copy, not the right to use.

  In any event, there is a version of RHEL3 free from RedHat copyrighted
material - it's called White Box Enterprise Linux. There's no violation
of copyright when this distribution is used. 
 
> 
> think of a user, say a music composer. He
> wants to setup a small home studio where he can do some recording and
> arrangements.. there is no way on earth that he can think of something
> like linux..
>
  You are mistaken - a lot of independent music studios and groups are
in fact using Linux for their digital studio setups. Reason? There are
tools for converting from virtually any format to any other format
imaginable, apart from outstanding driver support.

-Siva

From gopu.bhaskar at wipro.com  Thu Oct 21 12:26:21 2004

Subject: [Ilugc] How the linux kernel is superior than windows
Message-ID: <9FF1E2261921B84C942BD10365DBFC9C01BAF6B4@chn-gdy-msg.wipro.com>


> Strictly speaking, no.
>

Strictly speaking winnt kernel should not be called a monolithic kernel.
In the case Linux the kernel is like a singe a.out file, of course you
have the option of adding kernel modules at run time, but that still
boils down to adding a few procedures to this single a.out.
But if you look at the WinNT kernel you have a hardware abstraction
layer implemented in a dll, an Executive that does various management
duties, a Win32 subsystem and many more subsystems that communicate with
each other using a Client/Server mechanism. They have also borrowed from
a layered approach in their design.
This combination of layered and client/server approach is the basic
difference between the linux kernel and the winnt kernel. It was
generally believed that monolithic kernels are better in performance
than micro kernels. The following discussion between Tanenbaum and Linus
would be interesting read, if you have not already read it !
http://www.dina.dk/~abraham/Linus_vs_Tanenbaum.html

Please visit the following link for an overview of the window NT kernel
architecture
http://www.softlookup.com/tutorial/winnt/ch02.asp


> http://groups-
>
beta.google.com/group/comp.os.linux.advocacy/browse_thread/thread/81f308
b3

>
> On Thu, 2004-10-21 at 10:42, gopu.bhaskar@wipro.com wrote:
> > >
> > > Both are monolithic in terms of design.
> > >
> >
> > Are you sure about this? I remember reading somewhere that Windows
uses
> > a modified _microkernel_ (hybrid kernel).

Has anyone tried using the Plan 9 Operating System at
http://cm.bell-labs.com ?  Recently, Sivasankar pointed to the Rob Pike
interview at /., that inturn has links to Acme editor which is also done
by Rob Pike.

The Acme text editor manual says: "The most important distinction is
just that EMACS is fundamentally keyboard- based, while Acme is
[fundamentally?] mouse-based."  Acme manual describes itself as a text
based window system, shell & editor. But please don't imagine the bells
and whistles of usual gui applications there.  There are plenty of fresh
new ideas in there, though built upon from good old emacs.

BTW, Plan 9 OS is released under the Lucent Public License as free software.

I sympathize for your ignorance. If you look into the
past archives of our mailing list you will find lot of
ads for Linux kernel engineers, device driver
programmers etc. These guys are paid big and there is
lot of demand even in huge companies like IBM, HP, et.
al.

Linux is used a lot in the Embedded Industry, for
example. Linux for a VLSI company makes more sense
because it is very useful to prove that their hardware
works to specification, rather than spend $$$ for a
closed source OS that you can't rely on. By the way,
embedded systems is one of the hottest industries
today and there is a lot to gain from the market.

> remember also that a
> > majority of Linux distros that are easily usables
> by most users are
> > paid versions. 

Yes, at an affordable cost. RMS was not against
business, he was for ethical business. 

Why would an end user pay USD $300 for an OS like
windoze that crashed often, when you can get reliable
SuSE Linux, with documentation and tech support for
USD $89?

My thoughts,

Ramanraj K wrote:

> Has anyone tried using the Plan 9 Operating System at
> http://cm.bell-labs.com ?  Recently, Sivasankar pointed to the Rob Pike
> interview at /., that inturn has links to Acme editor which is also done
> by Rob Pike.
>
I've had it installed on my machine for a long time. Somehow I found 
myself so pampered with the Linux/*BSD environment that I could not 
embrace Plan 9 as my full time operating environment. There were not 
many applications on it, and its a little hard to get accustomed
with its user interface (a lot of functionality being bound to the mouse 
and cannot be substituted with keyboard shortcuts).

Lack of applications and support for hardware devices makes it 
impractical for day to day usage. Its a good research  OS though, and I 
believe that a lot of operating systems will derive concepts from it.

Working on  Plan 9 environment (Acme, even) reminded me of Dennis 
Ritchie's infamous 'ed' philosophy -  Keep it so simple that only a user 
who "knows" it can use.

Its a nice OS to play with, just for kicks.

Cheers,
Chandrashekar Babu.

> closed source OS that you can't rely on. By the way,
> 

Linux (or *ix) for VLSI makes sense, because the two major development
suites (Cadence and Synopsis) are for the *ix platform (correct
me if I am wrong). Several companies use Solaris (another closed
source OS). The cost of these tools is of several orders of
magnitude greater than the cost of the operating system.

Only big companies like Intel, IBM can afford to develop their
own tools. Most companies use either Synopsis or Cadence. I have
heard that ARM has only three licenses for a particular tool in
this suite and only three can work on the same tool
simultaneously. I can't confirm that this information is correct. 

Suriya

From ksriram at gmx.net  Fri Oct 22 16:31:08 2004
From: ksriram at gmx.net (Sriram Krishnan)
Date: Fri Oct 22 16:30:02 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] SELinux, ACLs, etc.
In-Reply-To: <20041021022935.28CDA329BE@whitebox.dias.ath.cx>
Message-ID: <20041022105955.E3BA11DB268@www.ae.iitm.ac.in>

Well - sorry that I took some time in replying to this post. I'll forward
some of your points along (as I'm totally clueless on the inner workings on
the kernel). However, I do have a few observations to make


>   It's better to migrate the apps methodically to an SELinux 
> policy-based mechanism, rather than change everything 
> overnight, as Longhorn is attempting to do. Over time, the 
> Linux model will work better, as it offers a larger ensemble 
> of choices, including whether to use SELinux or not.
>

We can debate for eternity whether this is better or not. What you've raised
is a fundamental difference between the way Windows works and Linux works
(as in the fact that there's only company developing Windows which controls
what goes in and what doesn't). So this isn't really a technical point and
more of a standard OSS vs Microsoft thing - so its better if I avoid it

> > 
> > Windows NT supports pluggable
> > authentication models.  ....<snip>
> > 
>   ACLs, PAM, Kerberos, etc. are all derived from OSS- and 
> Unix implementations, and ...<snip>

Hmm..and there's a lot more on Linux's ACL models. However, you've missed
the point. To quote from your original post

>>There is no option on Windows platform infact, to replace the default MAC
security model with DBAC, RSBAC, FLASK or anything better. 

So what he was trying to clear up here is that replacing the authentication
model *is* possible. This mail does *not* attempt to compare or deride the
technical capabilities Linux kernel in any way - but just stresses that
Windows has a way of plugging in authentication models, unlike stated in the
original mail. 

However, I have to admit I screwed up. When I sent the mail to that
Microsoft chap, he didn't realize would be forwarded exactly to a group. I
should have probably snipped out some of the parts which questioned Linux
and didn't deal with authentication in Windows. For that, I apologize - I
should have tried to keep this as technical as possible

Sriram

 http://rpmseek.com/rpm/popt-1.7-176.i586.html?cx=0:-:0:1502430:0:0:0

 Next time when you're missing something..
 try  http://rpmseek.com/

Kumaraguru

From lug at chandrashekar.info  Fri Oct 22 23:33:36 2004
From: lug at chandrashekar.info (Chandrashekar Babu)
Date: Fri Oct 22 23:33:47 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] Ubuntu Any one?
In-Reply-To: <3b020fee04102210364bc6875d@mail.gmail.com>
References: <3b020fee04102210364bc6875d@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <41794B78.8040207@chandrashekar.info>

Sivanandhan, P. wrote:

>Hi Fellows does any one got latest Ubuntu Linux CDs? (Version 4.10
>AFAIK). I've seen lot of people talking about this, seems to be a hard
>rock. I got impressed with it even b4 using it.
>  
>
I just downloaded Ubuntu Linux last week, installed and worked with it 
for a while. If you are fond of Debian+Gnome, then you might like this 
distro a lot. The user interface is polished and more simplified for the 
end users (Gnome 2.8). But it was somewhat slow on my machine (AthlonXP 
1800+, 256MB RAM, KT266 chip motherboard) when compared to Gnome 2.6.1 
on Slackware 10.0.

However, it might appeal to end users. No development tools (gcc 
toolchain) are bundled on the CDROM. The installer resembled Debian very 
much, except that its a lot simplified (lesser number of questions asked!).

By the way, when I boot Ubuntu Linux on a VIA C3 733 MHz/256 MB 
RAM/SiS630E chipset motherboard, it hangs with a blank screen. Anyone 
(by a vague chance) with the same hardware configuration as mine, facing 
this problem ?

Cheers,
Chandrashekar Babu.

user can WYSIWYG edit xml documents.  The styling elements can be provided by
css/xsl-fo.  If any one knows better, please drop me a line.

From karthick.ramachandran at gmail.com  Mon Oct 25 18:51:54 2004
From: karthick.ramachandran at gmail.com (R Karthick)
Date: Mon Oct 25 18:52:23 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] How to Access windows partition from RH 9.0?
In-Reply-To: <BC6E2BCE16A9D511933F00508BAED7C30969E92B@CTPNT003>
References: <BC6E2BCE16A9D511933F00508BAED7C30969E92B@CTPNT003>
Message-ID: <7e0d5bce0410250621153d2dd0@mail.gmail.com>

mount -t <fstype> /dev/hd<partition> <target directory>

eg:

$mount -t vfat /dev/hda1 /mnt/c

it is assumed above that the  partition is fat32 in windows2000 and a
dir named /mnt/c exists :-)

Read The Man for more 

$man mount

HTH,
R K

> -----Original Message-----
> From: ilugc-bounces@ae.iitm.ac.in 
> [mailto:ilugc-bounces@ae.iitm.ac.in] On Behalf Of R Karthick
> Sent: 25 October 2004 18:52
> To: Chakravarthi_Mohan
> Cc: chennailug
> Subject: Re: [Ilugc] How to Access windows partition from RH 9.0?
> 
> mount -t <fstype> /dev/hd<partition> <target directory>
> 
> eg:
> 
> $mount -t vfat /dev/hda1 /mnt/c
> 
> it is assumed above that the  partition is fat32 in 
> windows2000 and a dir named /mnt/c exists :-)
> 
> Read The Man for more 
> 
> $man mount
> 
> HTH,

=====
Beware 'Fedora-Redhat' Fake Security Alert
Posted by timothy on Sunday October 24, @08:52PM

from the don't-get-took dept.

rixdaffy writes "I just received an email from the 'Redhat Security
Team' telling me that I needed to download some tar file from
fedora-redhat.com. Besides the fact that I don't use Red Hat/Fedora, I
immediately smelled something fishy. Maybe it's not the first trojan
targeted at Linux users, but together with the official sounding
domain, it could trick some users into downloading and running the
binary. It looks like Red Hat is already aware of the issue."
According to Red Hat's page, "These emails tell users to download and
run an update from a users home directory. This fake update appears to
contain malicious code."

From rsubr at pcomm.hfi.unimelb.edu.au  Mon Oct 25 23:03:11 2004
From: rsubr at pcomm.hfi.unimelb.edu.au (Raja Subramanian)
Date: Mon Oct 25 22:51:38 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] Regarding CVS frontends and CVS
In-Reply-To: <20041025163031.53174.qmail@web54106.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20041025163031.53174.qmail@web54106.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20041025173311.GA7233@pcomm.hfi.unimelb.edu.au>

ignacius nallu wrote:
> Dear friends,
> Do any of you use CVS for version control in your Office?
> In our office we are switching from VSS to CVS...
> I configured CVS in Linux server and accessed the Repository,everything works fine...
> But CVS doesnt provide Reserved checkouts by default.It can be mad
> eavailable by using set of cvs commands[cvs checkout,editor,lock,edit-for
> checkout ; cvs commit,unlock for checkin]..

IIRC, CVSNT has something ("edit -c") that could do what you are asking.

But do you really want reserved checkouts?  CVS' main advantage is that
several developers can work on the same code and CVS can intelligently merge
their changes.  Locking defeats all this.


> I got this idea from cvsfe from ccvs.org site....
> We use gcvs in linux and wincvs in windows...[currently we are using open
> ssh for cvs client side access]

Also have a look at Windows CVS client - www.tortisecvs.org.  It's usage
simpler than WinCVS and probably supports CVSNT's locking flag.

Pserver on linux with tortise/wincvs on clients is probably the best way to
deploy CVS on a mixed Unix/Win environment.

- Raja

From kums at gmx.net  Tue Oct 26 01:18:38 2004

Hi,

Sivasankar Chander wrote:
>   Which leads to a fairly simple countermeasure - always recommended,
> rarely implemented: uninstall the compilers on production servers.

I'm not sure if that buys you much protection... you can always obtain a
binary for the target hardware.  To protect against rogue users, I used to
religiously uninstall compilers on all production boxes.  This was until I
realised how easily binaries for the target hardware can be obtained.
This holds true even for non-x86 platforms running proprietary unices.

- Raja

From siva at dias.ath.cx  Wed Oct 27 08:38:25 2004
From: siva at dias.ath.cx (Sivasankar Chander)
Date: Wed Oct 27 09:04:28 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: [security] Trojan attack launched against RedHat
	Fedora users

Message-ID: <20041027030825.5EDDB329BE@whitebox.dias.ath.cx>

> 
> I'm not sure if that buys you much protection... you can always obtain a
> binary for the target hardware.  To protect against rogue users, I used to
> religiously uninstall compilers on all production boxes.  This was until I
> realised how easily binaries for the target hardware can be obtained.
> 
  Cross-compiled binaries may be available, but more difficult to sneak
in as a trojan. This attack was disguised as a patch.

  Uninstalling compilers won't stop all attacks, but will stop some attacks.
If you're not going to be building stuff on the production server, it's
probably best to uninstall compilers.

  Chroot/jail-type setups are even better, but it takes more effort to
configure.

-Siva

Sridhar R <sridharinfinity@gmail.com> writes:
> Have a look at the website for features
> list:http://subversion.tigris.org/

<quote>
# Directories, renames, and file meta-data are versioned.  

Subversion versions not only file contents and file existence, but
also directories, copies, and renames.
</quote>

That is great., this simply means one does not have to plan the
entire layout of his source directory tree before starting a
project.

<quote>
# Commits are truly atomic.

succeeded. Revision numbers are per-commit, not per-file; log
messages are attached to the revision, not stored redundantly as
</unquote>

Means easier to look through logs. Wont get confused with all the
logs.. wont have to check the logs of every file to see where I
went wrong. This is interesting..

-------- Original Message --------
Subject: [Fsf-friends] Re: DotNet
Date: Wed, 27 Oct 2004 06:48:28 -0400
From: Richard Stallman <rms@gnu.org>
Reply-To: rms@gnu.org
To: Frederick Noronha (FN) <fred@bytesforall.org>
CC: fsf-friends@gnu.org.in
References: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0410260254570.1123@news.goa.bytesforall.org>

 >Re the request by Microsoft to speak at the Hyderabad usergroup on 
 >DotNet,

I am not sure what the "Hyderabad usergroup" means, but anyone who
advocates freedom should not offer a proprietary software developer a
platform to present a practical discussion of a proprietary software
product.

Such discussion focuses on the practical characteristics of the
software, and therefore makes the implicit assertion that, "There is
no ethical issue with this software, therefore the interesting things
about it are its technical capabilities and merits/demerits."  We need
to reject that assumption, and the best way is not to offer them a
platform at all.

_______________________________________________
Fsf-friends mailing list

From siva at dias.ath.cx  Wed Oct 27 20:42:52 2004
From: siva at dias.ath.cx (Sivasankar Chander)

> 
> Subject: [Fsf-friends] Re: DotNet
> Date: Wed, 27 Oct 2004 06:48:28 -0400
> From: Richard Stallman <rms@gnu.org>
> Reply-To: rms@gnu.org
> 
>  >Re the request by Microsoft to speak at the Hyderabad usergroup on 
>  >DotNet,
> 
> I am not sure what the "Hyderabad usergroup" means, but anyone who
> advocates freedom should not offer a proprietary software developer a
> platform to present a practical discussion of a proprietary software
> product.
> 
  While I respect RMS and his opinions on this matter, I must
disagree with this particular opinion. There's no harm (and in most 
cases, some benefit) from listening to alternative viewpoints, even if
it conflicts with the views held by the majority of the constituency
it is addressed to. There's always something to learn - they should
allow the MS rep to speak at the LUG. Having a closed mind reveals
weakness. Is the GNU philosophy so weak that it's going to lose adherents
as a result of a single presentation from the other camp? I sincerely
hope not.

  At the very least, it's worth knowing what their strategy is wrt
to FLOSS, so as to be able to counter it more effectively (vintage
Arthashastra).

-Siva

Hi,

>>>>> "DK" == Dileep M Kumar <kumarayil@asianetindia.com> writes:

    >> If anybody has serious objections (that is they DONT want the
    >> >Microsoft person to speak) regarding this please let me know.

    DK> I do like to point out that in a not-so-recent interview to
    DK> one of the computer magazines in India (forgot which, might
    DK> have been PCQuest or something), Microsoft India's
    DK> some-big-shot mentioned (not in so many words) that part of
    DK> their strategy in India is to "infiltrate" the LUGs and slowly
    DK> brainwash these LUGs into being MUGs (Microsoft User Groups -
    DK> pun intended :) . Let us not be the first or only LUG to fall
    DK> into that trap.

My two cents.  If someone from MS is going to talk about an MS only
product or MS only tools that are not OSS (and have no direct
relevance to the lug), however technical it might be, I think the
answer should be NO.  Same goes for a Mac.  IMHO, that is completely
off-topic for a LUG.  LUGs are not only-tech-groups.  There are
certain philosophies and common sense rules that we do follow.

As regards MS's strategy to 'brainwash' lug members.  Hehe, nice try.
Its dumb plans like these that really get my rolling my eyes over.

The significant thing about OSS is that the bar to entry is very low
and the distribution mechanisms allow me to scratch my itch and let
others use the tools I built in that process to scratch theirs.  Its a
community from the ground up.  Any community that is based on a policy
of unmotivated mutual help and sharing builds an extremely strong and
robust emotional bond.  The instant you bring a company to the party
that does not understand this, the company will be simply ignored like
an outsider in a family affair.

This is simply not a community that revolves around a mega-corp whose
raison d'etre is to make a profit and please its shareholders.  This
is the definition of a successful company and MS is probably one of
the most successful ones around.  So if MUGs are to be as successful
and meaningful to their members as LUGs then MSFT would cease to exist
(atleast in its current form).  Heads I win, tails you lose.

cheers,
prabhu

these people care.They care deeply. Microsoft has guys in Bangalore whose
only job is to help out the communities - but without getting in their way.
You have a problem with a product? They'll put you in touch with the product
team. And these guys love their job. 

I know someone (http://deepakg.blogspot.com) who gets cheered and wild
applause whenever he goes on stage. He cares deeply and he loves Microsoft
as much as you love OSS. And so does the current guy in charge
(http://abhishekkant.blogspot.com) who on an average visits around 5 cities
a week. Forget Microsoft employees. Take someone like me. I care. Am I a MS
employee? No. Am I going to work for them? Nope - I've already been placed
in CTS and will join them when I leave college. But I love the company and
their products. And there are a lot of people like me. Other employees
believe strongly enough to post on their blog about this very
thread!(http://pensieve.thinkingms.com/PermaLink,guid,efbb32ca-7b46-4b7d-96e
b-8ff3ff090372.aspx)

Guys - don't get me wrong. Passion and philosophy is not your domain alone.
I have some strong views on what is right and what is wrong and defend it
the same way you do.And there are a *lot* of people like me. I find it funny
when I'm called a M$ spy or an evil ranter. What would you think if you
defended Linux on my group and someone called you a Redhat spy..or some
other derogatory term?

 So if MUGs are to be as successful and meaningful to 
> their members as LUGs then MSFT would cease to exist (atleast in its 
> current form).  Heads I win, tails you lose.

Hmm..forget Microsoft communities for the moment. Communities have existed
around proprietary products for decades -Apple fans are the most hardcore of
the lot. Also, check out Usenet, which is sprawling with
communities.Whenever you have a product that a bunch of people use, you'll
always have the people getting together and sharing tips and helping each
other. 

**The unique thing about OSS is that the people also own the product- and it
is *their* product**. But tell any Mac fan that Apple products suck and
you'll probably wind up with a black eye.

As for Microsoft communities, there are tons of communities around. I run
one for students (www.cnugstudent.net) which has got over 750 members in the
last 8 months. The Bangalore group (http://groups.msn.com/bdotnet) has over
3000 members and the traffic is just overwhelming.Microsoft is getting
involved in these communities only very recently (say last year) - but they
have a hands-off approach. I can do a Mono or a DotGnu session tommorow and
no one will question me or criticize me (and in fact, I've run Linux during
my sessions several times to show off interoperability, Mono,etc.).

And these communities contribute bigtime. Edit-and-continue was implemented
for C# 2.0 at a very late stage for the sole reason that a lot of people
asked for it. Everywhere, you'll find MS employees looking for people using
their products. If you're a programmer, don't you want to talk to the people
who use your code? See how they use it? What problems they face? And fix
those problems? The same thing motivates the Microsoft developers. They're
hackers - just like a lot of you. Yes - in OSS, you could go fix your
problems yourself. But we're talking of a different world here - and saying
that one is better than another would just lead to another philosophy war.

Saying Microsoft sucks is one thing - but underestimate their communities
and you'll be underestimating a *lot* of very smart people who swear and
live by these products.Dont believe me? Check out www.ineta.org or
microsoft.public.* newsgroups.

There are a lot of people who care as much as you guys do.

You guys own your product - you can make any changes you want. I respect and
admire that. But there are a lot of people like me who believe in other
things. Don't deride us.



Sriram

From siva at dias.ath.cx  Thu Oct 28 16:33:25 2004
From: siva at dias.ath.cx (Sivasankar Chander)
Date: Thu Oct 28 16:59:29 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] [OT] Engineers - Produce patents, products...
In-Reply-To: <20041028094511.92119.qmail@web14827.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20041028110325.1EDBA329BE@whitebox.dias.ath.cx>

>
> Does Indian educational system teach engineers to make products,
> get patents, do research ?
>  
<rant>

  I'll deal with each of these activities separately - they're
entirely different activities, usually performed by different groups
of people, usually called engineers, lawyers and scientists.

  First off, the Indian educational system does not teach *anybody* to
make products, so stop picking on engineers. The main purpose of the
British-Indian educational system (which continues unchanged to date)
was to produce clerical staff with skills to peruse documents, add
numbers and recite regulations from memory. It does this very well,
and that's the main reason there's a huge inflow of BPO work over the
last few years. There are actually very few people in India who have
any aptitude for product engineering at all - so it's probably just
as well that most engineers switch to BPO, web-design, etc. It's
probably a good thing that they aren't let loose to design bridges,
cars, aircraft, etc. (There are, of course, a few very good engineers
who actually build stuff that works - in ISRO, DAE, etc., but it took
decades to build those skills, and it happened in spite of the
educational system, not because of it).

  Secondly, getting patents has nothing to do with making products -
a quick glance at kind of patents issued by most patent offices around
the world (USPTO, etc.) would show that most patents are "defensive
patents" issued to large companies to build a patent portfolio. Why
do you need a patent portfolio? Basically to prevent any future attempt
at extortion from somebody who had nothing at all do with the invention
of the product. Typical examples are patents on the microprocessor,
RAM, etc., which were issued years *after* the invention to people who
were merely opportunistic enough to do the paperwork required to file
the patent. To prevent these opportunistic patents from blocking the
free and unfettered development of real products, most large companies
have huge patent and legal departments, which are usually not connected
to product development at all - in fact, the real engineers at the
companies I've worked were usually very reluctant to even deal with
the patent department. The patent department usually sits in a
different building, often in a different city, and is usually filled
with lawyers, not engineers, and they talk a different dialect of
English which is not well-understood outside of courtrooms, and
sometimes not well-understood even inside courtrooms.

  Thirdly, doing research has even less to do with product development
than filing patents. Again, the large companies I've worked at have a
different group doing research, and it's located away from the product
development teams. The better research groups (Bell Labs, IBM Research,
Xerox PARC, ...) actually have no obligation to develop anything that
remotely resembles a product - their mandate is usually free-wheeling,
'blue-sky' research. If they did not have that mandate, nobody worthwhile
would work there. In the event, each of these places has produced
ground-breaking original work, some of which has later found its way into
products - but that wasn't the intent of the people who came up with
the ideas. Most of the ideas don't ever see the light of day in an actual
product, usually because they just don't work. The people who sit in
research labs usually have math and science qualifications, not engineering
qualifications.

  The Indian educational system would actually do a half-way reasonable
job in grooming people for the second task - namely filing patents - mainly
because it is very similar to the kind of clerical work it was originally
designed to train people for. Not surprisingly, some patent-filing paper
work is actually moving to India as part of the BPO scenario.

  Most research remains firmly in US hands - the best science is performed
in a free-wheeling atmosphere, and only US labs have the resources and the
commitment to making this happen. Most attempts at re-creating this
elsewhere haven't worked well, and the reason is that very few people
actually understand what good science is. Throwing pots of money in an attempt
to do good science is usually futile. Moreover, there's no guarantee that
good science leads to good products - witness the erstwhile Soviet Union,
which by all evidence was outstanding at science, but pitifully poor at
product development. </rant>

  One of the important side-effects of OSS is that first time, the barrier
to entry to developing a useful software product is low enough for almost
anybody with the skills to be able to so. It's actually possible for
a good programmer/software engineer to develop a competitive SW product,
sitting almost anywhere in the world (you still need reasonable connectivity
to be able to do so effectively). Will Indians be able to seize the
opportunity and actually develop good, original OSS? It depends on
several things, not least of which is that there must be an aptitude for
product development, and the means to exercise that aptitude. We're
probably seeing the beginnings of this phenomenon over the last few
years, but there's still a long way to go.
 
-Siva

You would be shocked at the kind of interviewing that happens during campus
placement around engineering colleges. I attended one such *interview* by a
leading Indian IT company. Now, I have to admit that I was a bit
over-confident - I thought I was a sure shot to get in as every other person
being interviewed in my batch didn't know programming and weren't from IT or
CSc. 

Anyway, here's how it went. I walked into an empty class room and handed him
my resume. The guy kept it aside *without even looking* at it. So much for
impressing him with my Python prowess. I walked in expecting the first
question to be something like 'Tell me something about yourself' - but I got
totally kapowed.Here's how the conversation went (please do keep in mind
that this was a IT company hiring programmers) -

Question 1:(Wrote some strange chemical formula) What is that compound?
Me : No idea

Question  2: (drew a weird electrical circuit which I had never seen before)
What circuit is that?
Me : Err...sorry

Question 3:(Wrote some Physics law)That is the law of..blah..blah. Could you
derive it?
Me : I'm sorry - I remember studying this in 12th standard but just don't
remember the derivation now

(similar questioning for 20 minutes.Not *one* computer related question).


Compare this with how the questioning went for someone else who can code as
much as Vemkatesh Prasad can bat(same recruiter)

Recuriter: Hi there - show me your marksheets.

Recruiter: Wow..you got 200/200 in Chemistry in 12th? Tell me about it.

Recruiter: Thanks - you may leave.

Interview lasted for a grand total of 5 minutes and the guy got selected.

Now, I don't have anything against interviewers trying to be fair to all
streams of engineering - but this stikes me as unfair. I'm a coder - ask me
about coding!

Sriram

Shyam S wrote:

 > #ifdef linux
 > 	printf("This system is Linux\n");
 > #endif

Well, normally if one writes a portable code and if he wants to do some 
platforms specific tasks/checks, he would check for the macros "linux" 
or "__linux__" or variants.

> #ifdef gnu
> 	printf("This system is GNU\n");
> #endif	

GNU is not some single entity like Linux kernel. GNU is a collection of 
tools. You simply can't distinguish any single entity as GNU. GNU is a 
collection of tools/libraries on a *NIX variant platform. Maybe in 
GNU/Hurd you can check for "gnu" alone. I don't know.

I don't know what you want to check by looking for "gnu" macro. If glibc 
is what you are looking for, then check for the macro "__GLIBC__" 
instead of "gnu". So, "#ifdef gnu" should be "#ifdef __GLIBC__".
From kums at gmx.net  Fri Oct 29 00:41:32 2004
From: kums at gmx.net (Kumaraguru)
Date: Fri Oct 29 00:42:13 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] Why it give me Linux?
In-Reply-To: <41810691.3010908@symonds.net>
Message-ID: <000001c4bd21$f271ef20$176141db@kums>

Hi!

You can also define "gnu" at compile time like..

gcc -D gnu system.c

But if you want a built-in macro, you can use __GNUC__

#include <stdio.h>
main()
{
	#ifdef __GNUC__
		printf("This system is GNU C \n");
	#endif
	
	#ifdef linux
		printf("This system is Linux\n");
	#endif
}

Regards
Kumaraguru

Subject: [Ilugc] [OT] Engineers - Produce patents, products...

Sriram Krishnan wrote:

> Interview lasted for a grand total of 5 minutes and the guy got selected.
> 
> Now, I don't have anything against interviewers trying to be fair to all
> streams of engineering - but this stikes me as unfair. I'm a coder - ask me
> about coding!

The point is they look for good indepth understanding of any subject 
with good aptitude (not the Debian one ;-)) and problem solving skills. 
For freshers (campus recruits mostly), they donot look out for that 
excellent programming skills. If someone knows the basics of programming 
that is enough for them. In their training, they can always make some 
good problem solver a good programmer too. But the converse is not that 
easy. In my practical experience, I have seen a lot of non geeks (at 
college days) doing great in my industry compared to geeks. A typical 
example for such a sort of company is Infosys. They don't bother for 
your programming skills at all. They strictly believe in problem solving 
skills. Any computer graduate (B.Sc., B.C.A., etc) can write code. An 
engineer or a technograt is not needed for writing the code alone. There 
  are much more things for him to do except them. So, IMO there is 
nothing wrong with the recruitment process from the industry's perspective.

PS: I too was in the same view when I missed some companies in my campus 
interview (Anna university). Once you get into the industry, you will 
change your view too :-)

From sbharathi at MidasComm.Com  Fri Oct 29 13:03:19 2004
From: sbharathi at MidasComm.Com (Bharathi S)
Date: Fri Oct 29 12:42:50 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] How can i make my name server as a Squid server.
In-Reply-To: <41653f72041028235312c9936f@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.44.0410291256120.23126-100000@bharathi.midascomm.com>

On Fri, 29 Oct 2004, Siva Prakash wrote:

> But my client machines can't connect to the name/ SAMBA server. So i
> can't configure the send mail, (Mail server) because the sendmail use
> the name server for name resolution.

Add your ISP DNS in to your /etc/resolve.conf and try

nameserver UrServerIP
nameserver IspDnsIP1
nameserver IspDnsIP2

HTH :)
-- 
Bharathi S

Prabhu Ramachandran recently wrote:
 > Sorry about the junk test mails but I'm hoping this works so we don't
 > end up with null-body messages in the future.

and earlier wrote:
 > There was a problem with the HTML->text conversion that I've hopefully
 > fixed.  Try again and things should work (even without turning off

There may be other problems too that may be worth taking note.

I had the same problem earlier[1], with Mozilla, when I included a
portion of the "hello" program binary opened with emacs into the body of
the message, to make a point.  The reason might be that some junk [in
the sense, it makes no sense to us] characters may get included in the
body of the message, which some how instruct Mozilla to truncate the
message.  A simple test message, composed afresh, with one or two words,
entered only through the keyboard without cut and paste from
other programs, should help to rule out other errors.  Sangharsha may
also check the "Sent" folder, to confirm if truncated messages were
sent by Mozilla.

BTW, I am curious to know what Lord McCauley [Lord Macaulay?] spoke in
the British Parliament, just before English was introduced as a medium
of instruction in India, that Sangharsha has been trying to let this
list know. Many a legal system, including ours, is built upon the
philosophy of men like Jeremy Bentham and Lord Macaulay. If those
philosophers had had access to the tools we use today, the kind of legal
system we might be using may have been very different.  Very
unfortunately, with the kind of ridicule philosophy receives these days,
it may take eons before any useful reforms or changes become possible.

Before this is ticked off as OT, two bits of info:
Tim O'Reilly wrote [2]:
<quote>
I like to tell people the story of the Mechanical Turk, a 1770 hoax that
pretended to be a mechanical chess playing machine. The secret, of
course, was that a man was hidden inside. The Turk actually played a
small role in the history of computing. When Charles Babbage played
against the Turk in 1820 (and lost), he saw through the hoax, but was
moved to wonder whether a true computing machine would be possible.
</quote>

Then, Charles Babbage sought for funds from the British Parliament, to
build his mechanical computing device, at about the same time as
Macaulay was making his speeches, and this is well documented at
http://fourmilab.ch/babbage  Babbage recieved a little support
initially, but a lot of ridicule later.  His mother alone advised:
   "My dear son, you have advanced far in the accomplishment of a great
object, which is worthy of your ambition. You are capable of completing
it. My advice is--pursue it, even if it should oblige you to live on
bread and cheese."  Babbage never saw a fully working computing device,
but we are fortunate to live during a period when we can take much for
granted. The same issues that troubled Babbage still remain:  Can we 
please have a super computer like Thunder for our country ? AFAIK, that 
question has not even been debated in our Parliament yet. It is time we 
broke the repeating cycles of history to make substantial progress.

Cheers!
Ramanraj.

From siva at dias.ath.cx  Sat Oct 30 11:08:49 2004

> 
> I like to tell people the story of the Mechanical Turk, a 1770 hoax that
> pretended to be a mechanical chess playing machine. The secret, of
> course, was that a man was hidden inside.

  There's more to the story (which you can Google and obtain) - the man
inside was a short-statured, almost dwarf-like man, who also happened
to be one of the strongest Chess players in Europe at the time. I
think his name was Allgaier.

 Also, the Turk had a lot of panels which
would be opened several at a time to show that there was nobody inside,
but yet there were clever compartments that allowed the human inside to
stay out sight by moving from one place to another.

> 
> Can we 
> please have a super computer like Thunder for our country ? AFAIK, that 
> question has not even been debated in our Parliament yet.  
> 
  I don't think there's any really pressing need for a large clustered
machine in India - commodity PCs have become fast enough for most of the
problems that we need to solve (Moore's law took care of this automatically).
Those resources would be better spent on infrastructure - backbone fibre,
core and edge routers, metro ethernet, last-mile connectivity, etc. which
would leverage Metcalfe's law to bring the benefits of connectivity to a
larger proportion of the population (i.e. a large number of roads, US-style,
instead of one rusting power plant in the middle of nowhere, Enron-Dabhol-
Indian-style).

-Siva

From ramanraj at md4.vsnl.net.in  Sat Oct 30 18:56:40 2004
From: ramanraj at md4.vsnl.net.in (Ramanraj K)
Date: Sat Oct 30 20:38:09 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] Applications for Clusters
References: <20041030053849.8ADED329C4@whitebox.dias.ath.cx>
Message-ID: <41839690.2090707@md4.vsnl.net.in>

Sivasankar Chander wrote:

  >>Can we please have a super computer like Thunder for our country ?
  >>AFAIK, that question has not even been debated in our Parliament yet.
  >
  > I don't think there's any really pressing need for a large clustered
  > machine in India ...

Whether it is needed or not,  the fact that _we_ can debate it this way,
is revolutionary.  "Representative" form of democracy was thought of as
the most suitable for those times, but now Gandhian style of direct
decentralised, community based democracies are workable and possible,
but more on this down below. GNU mailman for public services may make
their functioning easier, to begin with. I do not have hard data that
supports the need for a Super Computer like Thunder, but many at the
india-egov list readily agreed, and it seems there is a pressing need
for smaller clusters with 4-10 nodes.  Every legislative Act has a bunch
of tables that are expected to be kept public and updated, and taking
them to the Internet in meaningful ways, keeping their ends in mind,
could easily use a super computer well.  Data for India's 100 crore
population in conjunction with the many public registers etc. may be one
of the most complex tasks where it could be used well.  Much of the data
may remain in local intranets, and may be queried by others, but for
effective decisions, a central server would be be a must, if the present
org style remains as it is.

More importantly, archiac procedures neet to be streamlined to avoid
GIGO disasters. Without any discussion about the architecture, we have a
lot of talk on implementations.  Now is the time to do serious
rethinking based on philosophy and normative ideals. If we manage to get
that right, every thing else should be fairly easy.

    > Those resources would be better spent on infrastructure -
    > backbone fibre, core and edge routers, metro ethernet,
    > last-mile connectivity, etc. which would leverage Metcalfe's law to
    > bring the benefits of connectivity to a larger proportion of the
    > population (i.e. a large number of roads, US-style,
    > instead of one rusting power plant in the middle of nowhere,
    > Enron-Dabhol-Indian-style).

Powers/Functions get split between Central|Federal, State and People,
and the networking between them is generally peer-to-peer.

There are important architectural differences between US and India:

US Style:           Encapsulation of Federal & State Functions.
                      Residue remains with the People
                      [vide IX & X Amendments of the US Const.]

Indian Style:       Mainly Central without strict encapsulation, and any
                      residue remains with the Parliament at the Centre.
                      [vide Art. 248 of the Indian Const.]

Much data under our system naturally gets centralised, and India
therefore needs and has more use for Thunder like super computers.  The
infrastructural network looks like getting done, and a strong central
super computer is missing to make good use of the nervous network
falling into place. Of course, wearing a watch does not automatically
mean punctuality, but if we desire punctuality as a virtue, a watch
could be of great help. So too, HPC could help to take us places. HTH.

-Ramanraj.

From siva at dias.ath.cx  Sat Oct 30 21:00:08 2004
From: siva at dias.ath.cx (Sivasankar Chander)
Date: Sat Oct 30 21:26:11 2004
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: The players behind the Turk
In-Reply-To: <1099138405.2105.207596248@webmail.messagingengine.com>
Message-ID: <20041030153008.974C4329C4@whitebox.dias.ath.cx>

> 
> > one of the strongest Chess players in Europe at the time. I
> > think his name was Allgaier.
> Correction: his name was Harry Nelson Pillsbury. 
> 
  According to Bill Wall, who has composed a page on Chess Automatons
at http://www.geocities.com/SiliconValley/Lab/7378/automat.htm,
Pillsbury was one of the operators behind a different machine, called
Ajeeb, that was built much later in 1868.

  Some of the operators behind the Turk, which was built in 1769, included
Jacques Mouret (nephew of Philidor, the greatest player of his time),
William Lewis (British champion), William Schlumberger (St Amant's teacher),
and Johann Allgaier (one of the strongest players in Europe, who was the
operator when it defeated Napolean Bonaparte in 1809 in 24 moves). Allgaier
was somewhat short-statured.

  There have been a number of fake Chess automatons built over the years,
but the first true chess automaton was built by a Spaniard, Torres Y Quevedo
only in 1890. Claude Shannon of Bell Labs also built an endgame machine
with electromechanical relays sometime in the 40s, prior to his seminal
work on tree searching (1949).

Recent OSS chess programs include GNUchess (descendant of the Northwestern Univ.
program that dominated computer chess in the 70s) and Crafty. Of course,
there are many commercial offerings as well. 

-Siva

Once we understand history and philosophy behind an action, then we can
easily understand the implementation in a better way.  BTW, I had only
read about Macaulay's well known speech when English was introduced as a
medium of intruction in India[2]:

"The destinies of our Indian Empire are covered with thick darkness....
   It may be that the public mind of India may expand under our system
till it has outgrown that system; that by good government we may educate
our subjects into a capacity for better government; that having become
instructed in European knowledge they may, in some future age, demand
European institutions.  Whether such a day will ever come, I know not.
But never will I attempt to avert or retard it.  Whenever, it comes, it
will be the proudest day in English history."

We should deal with all this history, the way Charles Babbage dealt with
the fake chess automaton - use it to build something better.  We are
free now, and on our own.

-Ramanraj.
